If you take a pit bull approach with another pit bull, you generally end up with a messy scene and lots of bruised feelings and resentment. Luckily, there‚Äôs another way without all the mess. It‚Äôs just four simple steps: 1. Use the late-night FM DJ voice. 2. Start with ‚ÄúI‚Äôm sorry . . .‚Äù 3. Mirror. 4. Silence. At least four seconds, to let the mirror work its magic on your counterpart. 5. Repeat. One of my students experienced the effectiveness of this simple process at her workplace, where her impulsive boss was known for his ‚Äúdrive-bys‚Äù: an infuriating practice by which the boss would suddenly swing by one‚Äôs office or cubicle unannounced with an ‚Äúurgent,‚Äù poorly thought out assignment that created a lot of unnecessary work. Past attempts at any kind of debate created immediate pushback. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs a better way‚Äù was always interpreted by this boss as ‚Äúthe lazy way.‚Äù Such a drive-by occurred toward the end of a long consulting engagement, one that had generated literally thousands of documents. The boss, still skeptical of anything ‚Äúdigital,‚Äù wanted the security of paper copies. Popping his head into her office, the boss said, ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs make two copies of all the paperwork.‚Äù ‚ÄúI‚Äôm sorry, two copies?‚Äù she mirrored in response, remembering not only the DJ voice, but to deliver the mirror in an inquisitive tone. The intention behind most mirrors should be ‚ÄúPlease, help me understand.‚Äù Every time you mirror someone, they will reword what they‚Äôve said. They will never say it exactly the same way they said it the first time. Ask someone, ‚ÄúWhat do you mean by that?‚Äù and you‚Äôre likely to incite irritation or defensiveness. A mirror, however, will get you the clarity you want while signaling respect and concern for what the other person is saying. ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù her boss responded, ‚Äúone for us and one for the customer.‚Äù ‚ÄúI‚Äôm sorry, so you are saying that the client is asking for a copy and we need a copy for internal use?‚Äù ‚ÄúActually, I‚Äôll check with the client‚Äîthey haven‚Äôt asked for anything. But I definitely want a copy. That‚Äôs just how I do business.‚Äù ‚ÄúAbsolutely,‚Äù she responded. ‚ÄúThanks for checking with the customer. Where would you like to store the in-house copy? There‚Äôs no more space in the file room here.‚Äù ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs fine. You can store it anywhere,‚Äù he said, slightly perturbed now. ‚ÄúAnywhere?‚Äù she mirrored again, with calm concern. When another person‚Äôs tone of voice or body language is inconsistent with his words, a good mirror can be particularly useful. In this case, it caused her boss to take a nice, long pause‚Äîsomething he did not often do. My student sat silent. ‚ÄúAs a matter of fact, you can put them in my office,‚Äù he said, with more composure than he‚Äôd had the whole conversation. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll get the new assistant to print it for me after the project is done. For now, just create two digital backups.‚Äù A day later her boss emailed and wrote simply, ‚ÄúThe two digital backups will be fine.‚Äù Not long after, I received an ecstatic email from this student: ‚ÄúI was shocked! I love mirrors! A week of work avoided!‚Äù Mirroring will make you feel awkward as heck when you first try it. That‚Äôs the only hard part about it; the technique takes a little practice. Once you get the hang of it, though, it‚Äôll become a conversational Swiss Army knife valuable in just about every professional and social setting. KEY LESSONS The language of negotiation is primarily a language of conversation and rapport: a way of quickly establishing relationships and getting people to talk and think together. Which is why when you think of the greatest negotiators of all time, I‚Äôve got a surprise for you‚Äîthink Oprah Winfrey. Her daily television show was a case study of a master practitioner at work: on a stage face-to-face with someone she has never met, in front of a crowded studio of hundreds, with millions more watching from home, and a task to persuade that person in front of her, sometimes against his or her own best interests, to talk and talk and keep talking, ultimately sharing with the world deep, dark secrets that they had held hostage in their own minds for a lifetime. Look closely at such an interaction after reading this chapter and suddenly you‚Äôll see a refined set of powerful skills: a conscious smile to ease the tension, use of subtle verbal and nonverbal language to signal empathy (and thus security), a certain downward inflection in the voice, embrace of specific kinds of questions and avoidance of others‚Äîa whole array of previously hidden skills that will prove invaluable to you, once you‚Äôve learned to use them. Here are some of the key lessons from this chapter to remember: ‚ñ† A good negotiator prepares, going in, to be ready for possible surprises; a great negotiator aims to use her skills to reveal the surprises she is certain to find. ‚ñ† Don‚Äôt commit to assumptions; instead, view them as hypotheses and use the negotiation to test them rigorously. ‚ñ† People who view negotiation as a battle of arguments become overwhelmed by the voices in their head. Negotiation is not an act of battle; it‚Äôs a process of discovery. The goal is to uncover as much information as possible. ‚ñ† To quiet the voices in your head, make your sole and all-encompassing focus the other person and what they have to say. ‚ñ† Slow. It. Down. Going too fast is one of the mistakes all negotiators are prone to making. If we‚Äôre too much in a hurry, people can feel as if they‚Äôre not being heard. You risk undermining the rapport and trust you‚Äôve built. ‚ñ† Put a smile on your face. When people are in a positive frame of mind, they think more quickly, and are more likely to collaborate and problem-solve (instead of fight and resist). Positivity creates mental agility in both you and your counterpart. There are three voice tones available to negotiators: 1. The late-night FM DJ voice: Use selectively to make a point. Inflect your voice downward, keeping it calm and slow. When done properly, you create an aura of authority and trustworthiness without triggering defensiveness. 2. The positive/playful voice: Should be your default voice. It‚Äôs the voice of an easygoing, good-natured person. Your attitude is light and encouraging. The key here is to relax and smile while you‚Äôre talking. 3. The direct or assertive voice: Used rarely. Will cause problems and create pushback. ‚ñ† Mirrors work magic. Repeat the last three words (or the critical one to three words) of what someone has just said. We fear what‚Äôs different and are drawn to what‚Äôs similar. Mirroring is the art of insinuating similarity, which facilitates bonding. Use mirrors to encourage the other side to empathize and bond with you, keep people talking, buy your side time to regroup, and encourage your counterparts to reveal their strategy.

 CHAPTER 3 DON‚ÄôT FEEL THEIR PAIN, LABEL IT It was 1998 and I was standing in a narrow hallway outside an apartment on the twenty-seventh floor of a high-rise in Harlem. I was the head of the New York City FBI Crisis Negotiation Team, and that day I was the primary negotiator. The investigative squad had reported that at least three heavily armed fugitives were holed up inside. Several days earlier the fugitives had used automatic weapons in a shoot-out with a rival gang, so the New York City FBI SWAT team was arrayed behind me, and our snipers were on nearby rooftops with rifles trained on the apartment windows. In tense situations like this, the traditional negotiating advice is to keep a poker face. Don‚Äôt get emotional. Until recently, most academics and researchers completely ignored the role of emotion in negotiation. Emotions were just an obstacle to a good outcome, they said. ‚ÄúSeparate the people from the problem‚Äù was the common refrain. But think about that: How can you separate people from the problem when their emotions are the problem? Especially when they are scared people with guns. Emotions are one of the main things that derail communication. Once people get upset at one another, rational thinking goes out the window. That‚Äôs why, instead of denying or ignoring emotions, good negotiators identify and influence them. They are able to precisely label emotions, those of others and especially their own. And once they label the emotions they talk about them without getting wound up. For them, emotion is a tool. Emotions aren‚Äôt the obstacles, they are the means. The relationship between an emotionally intelligent negotiator and their counterpart is essentially therapeutic. It duplicates that of a psychotherapist with a patient. The psychotherapist pokes and prods to understand his patient‚Äôs problems, and then turns the responses back onto the patient to get him to go deeper and change his behavior. That‚Äôs exactly what good negotiators do. Getting to this level of emotional intelligence demands opening up your senses, talking less, and listening more. You can learn almost everything you need‚Äîand a lot more than other people would like you to know‚Äîsimply by watching and listening, keeping your eyes peeled and your ears open, and your mouth shut. Think about the therapist‚Äôs couch as you read the following sections. You‚Äôll see how a soothing voice, close listening, and a calm repetition of the words of your ‚Äúpatient‚Äù can get you a lot further than a cold, rational argument. It may sound touchy-feely, but if you can perceive the emotions of others, you have a chance to turn them to your advantage. The more you know about someone, the more power you have. TACTICAL EMPATHY We had one big problem that day in Harlem: no telephone number to call into the apartment. So for six straight hours, relieved periodically by two FBI agents who were learning crisis negotiation, I spoke through the apartment door. I used my late-night FM DJ voice. I didn‚Äôt give orders in my DJ voice, or ask what the fugitives wanted. Instead, I imagined myself in their place. ‚ÄúIt looks like you don‚Äôt want to come out,‚Äù I said repeatedly. ‚ÄúIt seems like you worry that if you open the door, we‚Äôll come in with guns blazing. It looks like you don‚Äôt want to go back to jail.‚Äù For six hours, we got no response. The FBI coaches loved my DJ voice. But was it working? And then, when we were almost completely convinced that no one was inside, a sniper on an adjacent building radioed that he saw one of the curtains in the apartment move. The front door of the apartment slowly opened. A woman emerged with her hands in front of her. I continued talking. All three fugitives came out. None of them said a word until we had them in handcuffs. Then I asked them the question that was most nagging me: Why did they come out after six hours of radio silence? Why did they finally give in? All three gave me the same answer. ‚ÄúWe didn‚Äôt want to get caught or get shot, but you calmed us down,‚Äù they said. ‚ÄúWe finally believed you wouldn‚Äôt go away, so we just came out.‚Äù There is nothing more frustrating or disruptive to any negotiation than to get the feeling you are talking to someone who isn‚Äôt listening. Playing dumb is a valid negotiating technique, and ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt understand‚Äù is a legitimate response. But ignoring the other party‚Äôs position only builds up frustration and makes them less likely to do what you want. The opposite of that is tactical empathy. In my negotiating course, I tell my students that empathy is ‚Äúthe ability to recognize the perspective of a counterpart, and the vocalization of that recognition.‚Äù That‚Äôs an academic way of saying that empathy is paying attention to another human being, asking what they are feeling, and making a commitment to understanding their world. Notice I didn‚Äôt say anything about agreeing with the other person‚Äôs values and beliefs or giving out hugs. That‚Äôs sympathy. What I‚Äôm talking about is trying to understand a situation from another person‚Äôs perspective. One step beyond that is tactical empathy. Tactical empathy is understanding the feelings and mindset of another in the moment and also hearing what is behind those feelings so you increase your influence in all the moments that follow. It‚Äôs bringing our attention to both the emotional obstacles and the potential pathways to getting an agreement done. It‚Äôs emotional intelligence on steroids. As a cop in Kansas City, I was curious about how a select handful of veteran cops managed to talk angry, violent people out of fights or to get them to put down their knives and guns. When I asked how they did that, I rarely got more than a shrug. They couldn‚Äôt articulate what they did. But now I know the answer is tactical empathy. They were able to think from another person‚Äôs point of view while they were talking with that person and quickly assess what was driving them. Most of us enter verbal combat unlikely to persuade anyone of anything because we only know and care about our own goals and perspective. But the best officers are tuned in to the other party‚Äîtheir audience. They know that if they empathize, they can mold their audience by how they approach and talk to them. That‚Äôs why, if a corrections officer approaches an inmate expecting him to resist, he often will. But if he approaches exuding calm, the inmate will be much more likely to be peaceful. It seems like wizardry, but it‚Äôs not. It‚Äôs just that when the officer has his audience clearly in mind, he can become who he needs to be to handle the situation. Empathy is a classic ‚Äúsoft‚Äù communication skill, but it has a physical basis. When we closely observe a person‚Äôs face, gestures, and tone of voice, our brain begins to align with theirs in a process called neural resonance, and that lets us know more fully what they think and feel. In an fMRI brain-scan experiment,1 researchers at Princeton University found that neural resonance disappears when people communicate poorly. The researchers could predict how well people were communicating by observing how much their brains were aligned. And they discovered that people who paid the most attention‚Äîgood listeners‚Äîcould actually anticipate what the speaker was about to say before he said it. If you want to increase your neural resonance skills, take a moment right now and practice. Turn your attention to someone who‚Äôs talking near you, or watch a person being interviewed on TV. As they talk, imagine that you are that person. Visualize yourself in the position they describe and put in as much detail as you can, as if you were actually there. But be warned, a lot of classic deal makers will think your approach is softheaded and weak. Just ask former secretary of state Hillary Clinton. A few years ago during a speech at Georgetown University, Clinton advocated, ‚Äúshowing respect, even for one‚Äôs enemies. Trying to understand and, insofar as psychologically possible, empathize with their perspective and point of view.‚Äù You can predict what happened next. A gaggle of pundits and politicians pounced on her. They called her statement inane and na√Øve, and even a sign she had embraced the Muslim Brotherhood. Some said that she had blown her chances at a presidential run. The problem with all of that hot air is that she was right. Politics aside, empathy is not about being nice or agreeing with the other side. It‚Äôs about understanding them. Empathy helps us learn the position the enemy is in, why their actions make sense (to them), and what might move them. As negotiators we use empathy because it works. Empathy is why the three fugitives came out after six hours of my late-night DJ voice. It‚Äôs what helped me succeed at what Sun Tzu called ‚Äúthe supreme art of war‚Äù: to subdue the enemy without fighting. LABELING Let‚Äôs go back to the Harlem doorway for a minute. We didn‚Äôt have a lot to go on, but if you‚Äôve got three fugitives trapped in an apartment on the twenty-seventh floor of a building in Harlem, they don‚Äôt have to say a word for you to know that they‚Äôre worried about two things: getting killed, and going to jail. So for six straight hours in that sweltering apartment building hallway, the two FBI negotiating students and I took turns speaking. We rotated in order to avoid verbal stumbles and other errors caused by tiredness. And we stayed relentlessly on message, all three of us saying the same thing. Now, pay close attention to exactly what we said: ‚ÄúIt looks like you don‚Äôt want to come out. It seems like you worry that if you open the door, we‚Äôll come in with guns blazing. It looks like you don‚Äôt want to go back to jail.‚Äù We employed our tactical empathy by recognizing and then verbalizing the predictable emotions of the situation. We didn‚Äôt just put ourselves in the fugitives‚Äô shoes. We spotted their feelings, turned them into words, and then very calmly and respectfully repeated their emotions back to them. In a negotiation, that‚Äôs called labeling. Labeling is a way of validating someone‚Äôs emotion by acknowledging it. Give someone‚Äôs emotion a name and you show you identify with how that person feels. It gets you close to someone without asking about external factors you know nothing about (‚ÄúHow‚Äôs your family?‚Äù). Think of labeling as a shortcut to intimacy, a time-saving emotional hack. Labeling has a special advantage when your counterpart is tense. Exposing negative thoughts to daylight‚Äî‚ÄúIt looks like you don‚Äôt want to go back to jail‚Äù‚Äîmakes them seem less frightening. In one brain imaging study,2 psychology professor Matthew Lieberman of the University of California, Los Angeles, found that when people are shown photos of faces expressing strong emotion, the brain shows greater activity in the amygdala, the part that generates fear. But when they are asked to label the emotion, the activity moves to the areas that govern rational thinking. In other words, labeling an emotion‚Äîapplying rational words to a fear‚Äîdisrupts its raw intensity. Labeling is a simple, versatile skill that lets you reinforce a good aspect of the negotiation, or diffuse a negative one. But it has very specific rules about form and delivery. That makes it less like chatting than like a formal art such as Chinese calligraphy. For most people, it‚Äôs one of the most awkward negotiating tools to use. Before they try it the first time, my students almost always tell me they expect their counterpart to jump up and shout, ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you dare tell me how I feel!‚Äù Let me let you in on a secret: people never even notice. The first step to labeling is detecting the other person‚Äôs emotional state. Outside that door in Harlem we couldn‚Äôt even see the fugitives, but most of the time you‚Äôll have a wealth of information from the other person‚Äôs words, tone, and body language. We call that trinity ‚Äúwords, music, and dance.‚Äù The trick to spotting feelings is to pay close attention to changes people undergo when they respond to external events. Most often, those events are your words. If you say, ‚ÄúHow is the family?‚Äù and the corners of the other party‚Äôs mouth turn down even when they say it‚Äôs great, you might detect that all is not well; if their voice goes flat when a colleague is mentioned, there could be a problem between the two; and if your landlord unconsciously fidgets his feet when you mention the neighbors, it‚Äôs pretty clear that he doesn‚Äôt think much of them (we‚Äôll dig deeper into how to spot and use these cues in Chapter 9). Picking up on these tiny pieces of information is how psychics work. They size up their client‚Äôs body language and ask him a few innocent questions. When they ‚Äútell‚Äù his future a few minutes later, they‚Äôre really just saying what he wants to hear based on small details they‚Äôve spotted. More than a few psychics would make good negotiators for that very reason. Once you‚Äôve spotted an emotion you want to highlight, the next step is to label it aloud. Labels can be phrased as statements or questions. The only difference is whether you end the sentence with a downward or upward inflection. But no matter how they end, labels almost always begin with roughly the same words: It seems like . . . It sounds like . . . It looks like . . . Notice we said ‚ÄúIt sounds like . . .‚Äù and not ‚ÄúI‚Äôm hearing that . . .‚Äù That‚Äôs because the word ‚ÄúI‚Äù gets people‚Äôs guard up. When you say ‚ÄúI,‚Äù it says you‚Äôre more interested in yourself than the other person, and it makes you take personal responsibility for the words that follow‚Äîand the offense they might cause. But when you phrase a label as a neutral statement of understanding, it encourages your counterpart to be responsive. They‚Äôll usually give a longer answer than just ‚Äúyes‚Äù or ‚Äúno.‚Äù And if they disagree with the label, that‚Äôs okay. You can always step back and say, ‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt say that was what it was. I just said it seems like that.‚Äù The last rule of labeling is silence. Once you‚Äôve thrown out a label, be quiet and listen. We all have a tendency to expand on what we‚Äôve said, to finish, ‚ÄúIt seems like you like the way that shirt looks,‚Äù with a specific question like ‚ÄúWhere did you get it?‚Äù But a label‚Äôs power is that it invites the other person to reveal himself. If you‚Äôll trust me for a second, take a break now and try it out: Strike up a conversation and put a label on one of the other person‚Äôs emotions‚Äîit doesn‚Äôt matter if you‚Äôre talking to the mailman or your ten-year-old daughter‚Äîand then go silent. Let the label do its work. NEUTRALIZE THE NEGATIVE, REINFORCE THE POSITIVE Labeling is a tactic, not a strategy, in the same way a spoon is a great tool for stirring soup but it‚Äôs not a recipe. How you use labeling will go a long way in determining your success. Deployed well, it‚Äôs how we as negotiators identify and then slowly alter the inner voices of our counterpart‚Äôs consciousness to something more collaborative and trusting. First, let‚Äôs talk a little human psychology. In basic terms, people‚Äôs emotions have two levels: the ‚Äúpresenting‚Äù behavior is the part above the surface you can see and hear; beneath, the ‚Äúunderlying‚Äù feeling is what motivates the behavior. Imagine a grandfather who‚Äôs grumbly at a family holiday dinner: the presenting behavior is that he‚Äôs cranky, but the underlying emotion is a sad sense of loneliness from his family never seeing him. What good negotiators do when labeling is address those underlying emotions. Labeling negatives diffuses them (or defuses them, in extreme cases); labeling positives reinforces them. We‚Äôll come back to the cranky grandfather in a moment. First, though, I want to talk a little bit about anger. As an emotion, anger is rarely productive‚Äîin you or the person you‚Äôre negotiating with. It releases stress hormones and neurochemicals that disrupt your ability to properly evaluate and respond to situations. And it blinds you to the fact that you‚Äôre angry in the first place, which gives you a false sense of confidence. That‚Äôs not to say that negative feelings should be ignored. That can be just as damaging. Instead, they should be teased out. Labeling is a helpful tactic in de-escalating angry confrontations, because it makes the person acknowledge their feelings rather than continuing to act out. Early on in my hostage negotiation career, I learned how important it was to go directly at negative dynamics in a fearless but deferential manner. It was to fix a situation I‚Äôd created myself. I‚Äôd angered the top FBI official in Canada when I entered the country without first alerting him (so he could notify the Department of State), a procedure known as ‚Äúcountry clearance.‚Äù I knew I needed to call and assuage him to straighten out the situation, or I risked being expelled. Top guys like to feel on top. They don‚Äôt want to be disrespected. All the more so when the office they run isn‚Äôt a sexy assignment. ‚ÄúBless me, Father, for I have sinned,‚Äù I said when he answered the phone. There was a long pause at the other end of the line. ‚ÄúWho is this?‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúBless me, Father, for I have sinned,‚Äù I repeated. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs Chris Voss.‚Äù Again there was a long silence. ‚ÄúDoes your boss know you‚Äôre here?‚Äù I said he did, and crossed my fingers. At this point, the FBI official would have been completely within his rights to tell me to leave Canada immediately. But by mentioning the negative dynamic, I knew I‚Äôd diffused it as much as I could. I had a chance. ‚ÄúAll right, you‚Äôve got country clearance,‚Äù he finally said. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll take care of the paperwork.‚Äù Try this the next time you have to apologize for a bone-headed mistake. Go right at it. The fastest and most efficient means of establishing a quick working relationship is to acknowledge the negative and diffuse it. Whenever I was dealing with the family of a hostage, I started out by saying I knew they were scared. And when I make a mistake‚Äîsomething that happens a lot‚ÄîI always acknowledge the other person‚Äôs anger. I‚Äôve found the phrase ‚ÄúLook, I‚Äôm an asshole‚Äù to be an amazingly effective way to make problems go away. That approach has never failed me. Let‚Äôs go back to the cranky grandfather. He‚Äôs grumpy because he never sees the family and he feels left out. So he‚Äôs speaking up in his own dysfunctional way to get attention. How do you fix that? Instead of addressing his grumpy behavior, you acknowledge his sadness in a nonjudgmental way. You head him off before he can really get started. ‚ÄúWe don‚Äôt see each other all that often,‚Äù you could say. ‚ÄúIt seems like you feel like we don‚Äôt pay any attention to you and you only see us once a year, so why should you make time for us?‚Äù Notice how that acknowledges the situation and labels his sadness? Here you can pause briefly, letting him recognize and appreciate your attempts to understand what he‚Äôs feeling, and then turn the situation around by offering a positive solution. ‚ÄúFor us this is a real treat. We want to hear what you have to talk about. We want to value this time with you because we feel left out of your life.‚Äù Research shows that the best way to deal with negativity is to observe it, without reaction and without judgment. Then consciously label each negative feeling and replace it with positive, compassionate, and solution-based thoughts. One of my Georgetown University students, a guy named TJ, who worked as an assistant controller at the Washington Redskins, put that lesson to work while he was taking my negotiations class. The economy was in the toilet at the time, and Redskins season ticket holders were leaving in droves to avoid the cost. Worse, the team had been terrible the year before, and off-field player problems were alienating the fans. The team‚Äôs CFO was getting more worried‚Äîand cranky‚Äîby the day, and two weeks before the season was to start he walked by TJ‚Äôs desk and slammed down a folder full of paper. ‚ÄúBetter yesterday than today,‚Äù he said and walked away. Inside was a list of forty season ticket holders who hadn‚Äôt paid their bills, a USB drive with a spreadsheet about each one‚Äôs situation, and a script to use when calling them. TJ saw right away that the script was a disaster. It began by saying that his colleagues had been trying to call for months, and the account had been escalated to him. ‚ÄúI wanted to inform you,‚Äù it read, ‚Äúthat in order to receive your tickets for the upcoming season opener against the New York Giants, you will need to pay your outstanding balance in full prior to September 10.‚Äù It was the stupidly aggressive, impersonal, tone-deaf style of communication that is the default for most business. It was all ‚Äúme, me, me‚Äù from TJ, with no acknowledgment of the ticket holder‚Äôs situation. No empathy. No connection. Just give me the money. Maybe I don‚Äôt need to say it, but the script didn‚Äôt work. TJ left messages; no one called back. A few weeks into the class, TJ rewrote the script. These weren‚Äôt massive changes, and he didn‚Äôt offer the fans any discounts. What he did was add subtle tweaks to make the call about the fans, their situation, and their love of the team. Now the team was ‚ÄúYOUR Washington Redskins‚Äù and the purpose of the call was to ensure that the team‚Äôs most valuable fans‚Äîthe delinquent customers‚Äîwould be there at the season opener. ‚ÄúThe home-field advantage created by you each and every Sunday at FedEx Field does not go unnoticed,‚Äù TJ wrote. He then told them, ‚ÄúIn these difficult times, we understand our fans have been hit hard and we are here to work with you,‚Äù and asked the ticket holders to call back to talk through their ‚Äúunique situation.‚Äù Though superficially simple, the changes TJ made in the script had a deep emotional resonance with the delinquent ticket holders. It mentioned their debt to the team but also acknowledged the team‚Äôs debt to them, and by labeling the tough economic times, and the stress they were causing, it diffused the biggest negative dynamic‚Äîtheir delinquency‚Äîand turned the issue into something solvable. The simple changes masked a complex understanding of empathy on TJ‚Äôs side. With the new script, TJ was able to set up payment plans with all the ticket holders before the Giants game. And the CFO‚Äôs next visit? Well, it was far less terse. CLEAR THE ROAD BEFORE ADVERTISING THE DESTINATION Remember the amygdala, the part of the brain that generates fear in reaction to threats? Well, the faster we can interrupt the amygdala‚Äôs reaction to real or imaginary threats, the faster we can clear the road of obstacles, and the quicker we can generate feelings of safety, well-being, and trust. We do that by labeling the fears. These labels are so powerful because they bathe the fears in sunlight, bleaching them of their power and showing our counterpart that we understand. Think back to that Harlem landing: I didn‚Äôt say, ‚ÄúIt seems like you want us to let you go.‚Äù We could all agree on that. But that wouldn‚Äôt have diffused the real fear in the apartment, or shown that I empathized with the grim complexity of their situation. That‚Äôs why I went right at the amygdala and said, ‚ÄúIt seems like you don‚Äôt want to go back to jail.‚Äù Once they‚Äôve been labeled and brought into the open, the negative reactions in your counterpart‚Äôs amygdala will begin to soften. I promise it will shock you how suddenly his language turns from worry to optimism. Empathy is a powerful mood enhancer. The road is not always cleared so easily, so don‚Äôt be demoralized if this process seems to go slowly. The Harlem high-rise negotiation took six hours. Many of us wear fears upon fears, like layers against the cold, so getting to safety takes time. That was the experience of another one of my students, a fund-raiser for the Girl Scouts, who backed into naming her counterpart‚Äôs fears almost accidentally. We‚Äôre not talking about someone who sold Girl Scout cookies: my student was an experienced fund-raiser who regularly got donors to pony up $1,000 to $25,000 a check. Over the years, she‚Äôd developed a very successful system to get her ‚Äúclients,‚Äù usually wealthy women, to open their checkbook. She‚Äôd invite a potential donor to her office, serve a few Girl Scouts cookies, walk her through an album of heartwarming snapshots and handwritten letters from projects that matched the woman‚Äôs profile, and then collect a check when the donor‚Äôs eyes lit up. It was almost easy. One day, though, she met the immovable donor. Once the woman sat down in her office, my student began to throw out the projects her research had said would fit. But the woman shook her head at one project after another. My student found herself growing perplexed at the difficult donor who had no interest in donating. But she held her emotion in check and reached back to a lesson from my recent class on labeling. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm sensing some hesitation with these projects,‚Äù she said in what she hoped was a level voice. As if she‚Äôd been uncorked, the woman exclaimed: ‚ÄúI want my gift to directly support programming for Girl Scouts and not anything else.‚Äù This helped focus the conversation, but as my student put forth project after project that seemed to fulfill the donor‚Äôs criteria, all she got was still rejection. Sensing the potential donor‚Äôs growing frustration, and wanting to end on a positive note so that they might be able to meet again, my student used another label. ‚ÄúIt seems that you are really passionate about this gift and want to find the right project reflecting the opportunities and life-changing experiences the Girl Scouts gave you.‚Äù And with that, this ‚Äúdifficult‚Äù woman signed a check without even picking a specific project. ‚ÄúYou understand me,‚Äù she said as she got up to leave. ‚ÄúI trust you‚Äôll find the right project.‚Äù Fear of her money being misappropriated was the presenting dynamic that the first label uncovered. But the second label uncovered the underlying dynamic‚Äîher very presence in the office was driven by very specific memories of being a little Girl Scout and how it changed her life. The obstacle here wasn‚Äôt finding the right match for the woman. It wasn‚Äôt that she was this highly finicky, hard-to-please donor. The real obstacle was that this woman needed to feel that she was understood, that the person handling her money knew why she was in that office and understood the memories that were driving her actions. That‚Äôs why labels are so powerful and so potentially transformative to the state of any conversation. By digging beneath what seems like a mountain of quibbles, details, and logistics, labels help to uncover and identify the primary emotion driving almost all of your counterpart‚Äôs behavior, the emotion that, once acknowledged, seems to miraculously solve everything else. DO AN ACCUSATION AUDIT On the first day of negotiating class each semester, I march the group through an introductory exercise called ‚Äúsixty seconds or she dies.‚Äù I play a hostage-taker and a student has to convince me to release my hostage within a minute. It‚Äôs an icebreaker that shows me the level of my students, and it reveals to them how much they need to learn. (Here‚Äôs a little secret: the hostage never gets out.) Sometimes students jump right in, but finding takers is usually hard because it means coming to the front of the class and competing with the guy who holds all the cards. If I just ask for a volunteer, my students sit on their hands and look away. You‚Äôve been there. You can almost feel your back muscles tense as you think, Oh please, don‚Äôt call on me. So I don‚Äôt ask. Instead, I say, ‚ÄúIn case you‚Äôre worried about volunteering to role-play with me in front of the class, I want to tell you in advance . . . it‚Äôs going to be horrible.‚Äù After the laughter dies down, I then say, ‚ÄúAnd those of you who do volunteer will probably get more out of this than anyone else.‚Äù I always end up with more volunteers than I need. Now, look at what I did: I prefaced the conversation by labeling my audience‚Äôs fears; how much worse can something be than ‚Äúhorrible‚Äù? I defuse them and wait, letting it sink in and thereby making the unreasonable seem less forbidding. All of us have intuitively done something close to this thousands of times. You‚Äôll start a criticism of a friend by saying, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want this to sound harsh . . .‚Äù hoping that whatever comes next will be softened. Or you‚Äôll say, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to seem like an asshole . . .‚Äù hoping your counterpart will tell you a few sentences later that you‚Äôre not that bad. The small but critical mistake this commits is denying the negative. That actually gives it credence. In court, defense lawyers do this properly by mentioning everything their client is accused of, and all the weaknesses of their case, in the opening statement. They call this technique ‚Äútaking the sting out.‚Äù What I want to do here is turn this into a process that, applied systematically, you can use to disarm your counterpart while negotiating everything from your son‚Äôs bedtime to large business contracts. The first step of doing so is listing every terrible thing your counterpart could say about you, in what I call an accusation audit. This idea of an accusation audit is really, really hard for people to get their minds around. The first time I tell my students about it, they say, ‚ÄúOh my God. We can‚Äôt do that.‚Äù It seems both artificial and self-loathing. It seems like it would make things worse. But then I remind them that it‚Äôs exactly what I did the first day of class when I labeled their fears of the hostage game in advance. And they all admit that none of them knew. As an example, I‚Äôm going to use the experience of one of my students, Anna, because I couldn‚Äôt be more proud at how she turned what she learned in my class into $1 million. At the time, Anna was representing a major government contractor. Her firm had won a competition for a sizable government deal by partnering with a smaller company, let‚Äôs call it ABC Corp., whose CEO had a close relationship with the government client representative. Problems started right after they won the contract, though. Because ABC‚Äôs relationship had been instrumental in winning the deal, ABC felt that it was owed a piece of the pie whether it fulfilled its part of the contract or not. And so, while the contract paid them for the work of nine people, they continually cut back support. As Anna‚Äôs company had to perform ABC‚Äôs work, the relationship between ABC and Anna‚Äôs company fragmented into vituperative emails and bitter complaining. Facing an already low profit margin, Anna‚Äôs company was forced into tough negotiations to get ABC to take a cut to 5.5 people. The negotiations left a bitter aftertaste on both sides. The vituperative emails stopped, but then again all emails stopped. And no communication is always a bad sign. A few months after those painful talks, the client demanded a major rethink on the project and Anna‚Äôs firm was faced with losing serious money if it didn‚Äôt get ABC to agree to further cuts. Because ABC wasn‚Äôt living up to its side of the bargain, Anna‚Äôs firm would have had strong contractual grounds to cut out ABC altogether. But that would have damaged Anna‚Äôs firm‚Äôs reputation with a very important customer, and could have led to litigation from ABC. Faced with this scenario, Anna set up a meeting with ABC where she and her partners planned to inform ABC that its pay was being cut to three people. It was a touchy situation, as ABC was already unhappy about the first cut. Even though she was normally an aggressive and confident negotiator, worries about the negotiations ruined Anna‚Äôs sleep for weeks. She needed to extract concessions while improving the relationship at the same time. No easy task, right? To prepare, the first thing Anna did was sit down with her negotiating partner, Mark, and list every negative charge that ABC could level at them. The relationship had gone sour long before, so the list was huge. But the biggest possible accusations were easy to spot: ‚ÄúYou are the typical prime contractor trying to force out the small guy.‚Äù ‚ÄúYou promised us we would have all this work and you reneged on your promise.‚Äù ‚ÄúYou could have told us about this issue weeks ago to help us prepare.‚Äù Anna and Mark then took turns role-playing the two sides, with one playing ABC and the other disarming these accusations with anticipatory labels. ‚ÄúYou‚Äôre going to think we are a big, bad prime contractor when we are done,‚Äù Anna practiced saying slowly and naturally. ‚ÄúIt seems you feel this work was promised to you from the beginning,‚Äù Mark said. They trained in front of an observer, honing their pacing; deciding at what point they would label each fear; and planning when to include meaningful pauses. It was theater. When the day of the meeting arrived, Anna opened by acknowledging ABC‚Äôs biggest gripes. ‚ÄúWe understand that we brought you on board with the shared goal of having you lead this work,‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúYou may feel like we have treated you unfairly, and that we changed the deal significantly since then. We acknowledge that you believe you were promised this work.‚Äù This received an emphatic nod from the ABC representatives, so Anna continued by outlining the situation in a way that encouraged the ABC reps to see the firms as teammates, peppering her statements with open-ended questions that showed she was listening: ‚ÄúWhat else is there you feel is important to add to this?‚Äù By labeling the fears and asking for input, Anna was able to elicit an important fact about ABC‚Äôs fears, namely that ABC was expecting this to be a high-profit contract because it thought Anna‚Äôs firm was doing quite well from the deal. This provided an entry point for Mark, who explained that the client‚Äôs new demands had turned his firm‚Äôs profits into losses, meaning that he and Anna needed to cut ABC‚Äôs pay further, to three people. Angela, one of ABC‚Äôs representatives, gasped. ‚ÄúIt sounds like you think we are the big, bad prime contractor trying to push out the small business,‚Äù Anna said, heading off the accusation before it could be made. ‚ÄúNo, no, we don‚Äôt think that,‚Äù Angela said, conditioned by the acknowledgment to look for common ground. With the negatives labeled and the worst accusations laid bare, Anna and Mark were able to turn the conversation to the contract. Watch what they do closely, as it‚Äôs brilliant: they acknowledge ABC‚Äôs situation while simultaneously shifting the onus of offering a solution to the smaller company. ‚ÄúIt sounds like you have a great handle on how the government contract should work,‚Äù Anna said, labeling Angela‚Äôs expertise. ‚ÄúYes‚Äîbut I know that‚Äôs not how it always goes,‚Äù Angela answered, proud to have her experience acknowledged. Anna then asked Angela how she would amend the contract so that everyone made some money, which pushed Angela to admit that she saw no way to do so without cutting ABC‚Äôs worker count. Several weeks later, the contract was tweaked to cut ABC‚Äôs payout, which brought Anna‚Äôs company $1 million that put the contract into the black. But it was Angela‚Äôs reaction at the end of the meeting that most surprised Anna. After Anna had acknowledged that she had given Angela some bad news and that she understood how angry she must feel, Angela said: ‚ÄúThis is not a good situation but we appreciate the fact that you are acknowledging what happened, and we don‚Äôt feel like you are mistreating us. And you are not the ‚ÄòBig Bad Prime.‚Äô‚Äù Anna‚Äôs reaction to how this turned out? ‚ÄúHoly crap, this stuff actually works!‚Äù She‚Äôs right. As you just saw, the beauty of going right after negativity is that it brings us to a safe zone of empathy. Every one of us has an inherent, human need to be understood, to connect with the person across the table. That explains why, after Anna labeled Angela‚Äôs fears, Angela‚Äôs first instinct was to add nuance and detail to those fears. And that detail gave Anna the power to accomplish what she wanted from the negotiation. GET A SEAT‚ÄîAND AN UPGRADE‚ÄîON A SOLD-OUT FLIGHT Up to this point, we‚Äôve been building each skill as if they were musical instruments: first, try the saxophone mirror; now here‚Äôs the bass label; and finally, why don‚Äôt you blow a note on the French horn of tactical silence. But in a real negotiation the band all plays together. So you‚Äôve got to learn how to conduct. Keeping all the instruments playing is really awkward for most people. It seems to go by in such a rush. So what I‚Äôm going to do here is play a song at slow speed so you can hear each instrument note by note. I promise you‚Äôll quickly see how the skills you have been building play off one another, rising, riffing, falling, and pausing in perfect harmony. Here is the situation (the song, if you will): My student Ryan B. was flying from Baltimore to Austin to sign a large computer-consulting contract. For six months, the client representative had gone back and forth on whether he wanted the services, but a major system collapse put the representative in a tight spot with his CEO. To shift the blame, he called Ryan with his CEO on the line and very aggressively demanded to know why it was taking Ryan so long to come ink the contract. If Ryan was not there by Friday morning, he said, the deal was off. Ryan bought a ticket for the next morning, Thursday, but a freak lightning storm whipped up in Baltimore, closing the airport for five hours. It became painfully clear that Ryan wasn‚Äôt going to make his original connection to Austin from Dallas. Worse, when he called American Airlines just before departing, he found that his connection had been automatically rebooked to 3 p.m. the next day, putting the contract in jeopardy. When Ryan finally got to Dallas at 8 p.m., he ran to the gate where the day‚Äôs final American Airlines flight to Austin was less than thirty minutes from takeoff. His goal was to get on that flight or, at worst, get an earlier flight the next day. In front of him at the gate, a very aggressive couple was yelling at the gate agent, who was barely looking at them as she tapped on the computer in front of her; she was clearly making every effort not to scream back. After she‚Äôd said, ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs nothing I can do,‚Äù five times, the angry couple finally gave up and left. To start, watch how Ryan turns that heated exchange to his advantage. Following on the heels of an argument is a great position for a negotiator, because your counterpart is desperate for an empathetic connection. Smile, and you‚Äôre already an improvement. ‚ÄúHi, Wendy, I‚Äôm Ryan. It seems like they were pretty upset.‚Äù This labels the negative and establishes a rapport based on empathy. This in turn encourages Wendy to elaborate on her situation, words Ryan then mirrors to invite her to go further. ‚ÄúYeah. They missed their connection. We‚Äôve had a fair amount of delays because of the weather.‚Äù ‚ÄúThe weather?‚Äù After Wendy explains how the delays in the Northeast had rippled through the system, Ryan again labels the negative and then mirrors her answer to encourage her to delve further. ‚ÄúIt seems like it‚Äôs been a hectic day.‚Äù ‚ÄúThere‚Äôve been a lot of ‚Äòirate consumers,‚Äô you know? I mean, I get it, even though I don‚Äôt like to be yelled at. A lot of people are trying to get to Austin for the big game.‚Äù ‚ÄúThe big game?‚Äù ‚ÄúUT is playing Ole Miss football and every flight into Austin has been booked solid.‚Äù ‚ÄúBooked solid?‚Äù Now let‚Äôs pause. Up to this point, Ryan has been using labels and mirrors to build a relationship with Wendy. To her it must seem like idle chatter, though, because he hasn‚Äôt asked for anything. Unlike the angry couple, Ryan is acknowledging her situation. His words ping-pong between ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs that?‚Äù and ‚ÄúI hear you,‚Äù both of which invite her to elaborate. Now that the empathy has been built, she lets slip a piece of information he can use. ‚ÄúYeah, all through the weekend. Though who knows how many people will make the flights. The weather‚Äôs probably going to reroute a lot of people through a lot of different places.‚Äù Here‚Äôs where Ryan finally swoops in with an ask. But notice how he acts: not assertive or coldly logical, but with empathy and labeling that acknowledges her situation and tacitly puts them in the same boat. ‚ÄúWell, it seems like you‚Äôve been handling the rough day pretty well,‚Äù he says. ‚ÄúI was also affected by the weather delays and missed my connecting flight. It seems like this flight is likely booked solid, but with what you said, maybe someone affected by the weather might miss this connection. Is there any possibility a seat will be open?‚Äù Listen to that riff: Label, tactical empathy, label. And only then a request. At this point, Wendy says nothing and begins typing on her computer. Ryan, who‚Äôs eager not to talk himself out of a possible deal, engages in some silence. After thirty seconds, Wendy prints a boarding pass and hands it to Ryan, explaining that there were a few seats that were supposed to be filled by people who would now arrive much later than the flight‚Äôs departure. To make Ryan‚Äôs success even better, she puts him in Economy Plus seating. All that in under two minutes! The next time you find yourself following an angry customer at a corner store or airplane line, take a moment and practice labels and mirrors on the service person. I promise they won‚Äôt scream, ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt try to control me!‚Äù and burst into flames‚Äîand you might walk away with a little more than you expected. KEY LESSONS As you try to insert the tools of tactical empathy into your daily life, I encourage you to think of them as extensions of natural human interactions and not artificial conversational tics. In any interaction, it pleases us to feel that the other side is listening and acknowledging our situation. Whether you are negotiating a business deal or simply chatting to the person at the supermarket butcher counter, creating an empathetic relationship and encouraging your counterpart to expand on their situation is the basis of healthy human interaction. These tools, then, are nothing less than emotional best practices that help you cure the pervasive ineptitude that marks our most critical conversations in life. They will help you connect and create more meaningful and warm relationships. That they might help you extract what you want is a bonus; human connection is the first goal. With that in mind, I encourage you to take the risk of sprinkling these in every conversation you have. I promise you that they will feel awkward and artificial at first, but keep at it. Learning to walk felt awfully strange, too. As you internalize these techniques, turning the artifice of tactical empathy into a habit and then into an integral part of your personality, keep in mind these lessons from the chapter you‚Äôve just read: ‚ñ† Imagine yourself in your counterpart‚Äôs situation. The beauty of empathy is that it doesn‚Äôt demand that you agree with the other person‚Äôs ideas (you may well find them crazy). But by acknowledging the other person‚Äôs situation, you immediately convey that you are listening. And once they know that you are listening, they may tell you something that you can use. ‚ñ† The reasons why a counterpart will not make an agreement with you are often more powerful than why they will make a deal, so focus first on clearing the barriers to agreement. Denying barriers or negative influences gives them credence; get them into the open. ‚ñ† Pause. After you label a barrier or mirror a statement, let it sink in. Don‚Äôt worry, the other party will fill the silence. ‚ñ† Label your counterpart‚Äôs fears to diffuse their power. We all want to talk about the happy stuff, but remember, the faster you interrupt action in your counterpart‚Äôs amygdala, the part of the brain that generates fear, the faster you can generate feelings of safety, well-being, and trust. ‚ñ† List the worst things that the other party could say about you and say them before the other person can. Performing an accusation audit in advance prepares you to head off negative dynamics before they take root. And because these accusations often sound exaggerated when said aloud, speaking them will encourage the other person to claim that quite the opposite is true. ‚ñ† Remember you‚Äôre dealing with a person who wants to be appreciated and understood. So use labels to reinforce and encourage positive perceptions and dynamics.

 CHAPTER 4 BEWARE ‚ÄúYES‚Äù‚ÄîMASTER ‚ÄúNO‚Äù Let me paint a scenario we‚Äôve all experienced: You‚Äôre at home, just before dinner, and the phone rings. It is, no surprise, a telemarketer. He wants to sell you magazine subscriptions, water filters, frozen Argentine beef‚Äîto be honest, it doesn‚Äôt matter, as the script is always the same. After butchering your name, and engaging in some disingenuous pleasantries, he launches into his pitch. The hard sell that comes next is a scripted flowchart designed to cut off your escape routes as it funnels you down a path with no exit but ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù ‚ÄúDo you enjoy a nice glass of water from time to time.‚Äù ‚ÄúWell, yes, but . . .‚Äù ‚ÄúMe, too. And like me I bet you like crisp, clean water with no chemical aftertaste, like Mother Nature made it.‚Äù ‚ÄúWell, yes, but . . .‚Äù Who is this guy with a fake smile in his voice, you wonder, who thinks he can trick you into buying something you don‚Äôt want? You feel your muscles tighten, your voice go defensive, and your heart rate accelerate. You feel like his prey, and you are! The last thing you want to do is say ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù even when it‚Äôs the only way to answer, ‚ÄúDo you drink water?‚Äù Compromise and concession, even to the truth, feels like defeat. And ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù well, ‚ÄúNo‚Äù feels like salvation, like an oasis. You‚Äôre tempted to use ‚ÄúNo‚Äù when it‚Äôs blatantly untrue, just to hear its sweet sound. ‚ÄúNo, I do not need water, carbon filtered or otherwise. I‚Äôm a camel!‚Äù Now let‚Äôs think about this selling technique. It‚Äôs designed to get to ‚ÄúYes‚Äù at all costs, as if ‚ÄúNo‚Äù were death. And for many of us it is. We have all these negative connotations with ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù We talk about the rejection of ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù about the fear of hearing it. ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is the ultimate negative word. But at the end of the day, ‚ÄúYes‚Äù is often a meaningless answer that hides deeper objections (and ‚ÄúMaybe‚Äù is even worse). Pushing hard for ‚ÄúYes‚Äù doesn‚Äôt get a negotiator any closer to a win; it just angers the other side. So if ‚ÄúYes‚Äù can be so damn uncomfortable, and ‚ÄúNo‚Äù such a relief, why have we fetishized one and demonized the other? We have it backward. For good negotiators, ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is pure gold. That negative provides a great opportunity for you and the other party to clarify what you really want by eliminating what you don‚Äôt want. ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is a safe choice that maintains the status quo; it provides a temporary oasis of control. At some point in their development, all negotiators have to come to grips with ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù When you come to realize the real psychological dynamic behind it, you‚Äôll love the word. It‚Äôs not just that you lose your fear of it, but that you come to learn what it does for you and how you can build deals out of it. ‚ÄúYes‚Äù and ‚ÄúMaybe‚Äù are often worthless. But ‚ÄúNo‚Äù always alters the conversation. ‚ÄúNO‚Äù STARTS THE NEGOTIATION My fascination with ‚ÄúNo‚Äù in all its beautiful nuance began with a conversation I had a few months before my negotiation career began. I started my career with the Bureau as a member of the FBI SWAT team in the Pittsburgh Division but after nearly two years I was transferred to New York, where the FBI attached me to the Joint Terrorism Task Force (JTTF). It was an amazing post: We spent our days and nights tracking suspected terrorists, investigating their cells, and assessing whether or how they might strike. We were untying knots of human anger in the midst of America‚Äôs biggest city, making life-and-death decisions on who was dangerous and who was just blowing hot air. The work fascinated me. Ever since my first days with the Bureau, I had been obsessed with crisis response. The immediacy of the task enthralled me. The stakes were high. Lives hung in the balance. The emotional terrain was complex, changing, and often conflicting. To successfully gain a hostage‚Äôs safe release, a negotiator had to penetrate the hostage-taker‚Äôs motives, state of mind, intelligence, and emotional strengths and weaknesses. The negotiator played the role of bully, conciliator, enforcer, savior, confessor, instigator, and peacemaker‚Äîand that‚Äôs just a few of the parts. I thought I was cut out for every one of them. A few weeks after I got to Manhattan, I showed up at the desk of Amy Bonderow, who ran the FBI‚Äôs Crisis Negotiation Team in New York. I didn‚Äôt know beans about negotiating, so I went for the direct approach. ‚ÄúI want be a hostage negotiator,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúEveryone does‚Äîgot any training?‚Äù she asked. ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúAny credentials?‚Äù ‚ÄúNope.‚Äù I answered. ‚ÄúAny experience?‚Äù she asked. ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù I answered. ‚ÄúDo you have a degree in psychology, sociology, anything at all related to negotiation?‚Äù ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù ‚ÄúLooks like you answered your own question,‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúNo. Now go away.‚Äù ‚ÄúGo away?‚Äù I protested. ‚ÄúReally?‚Äù ‚ÄúYep. As in, ‚ÄòLeave me alone.‚Äô Everybody wants to be a hostage negotiator, and you have no r√©sum√©, experience, or skills. So what would you say in my position? You got it: ‚ÄòNo.‚Äô‚Äù I paused in front of her, thinking, This is not how my negotiating career ends. I had stared down terrorists; I wasn‚Äôt going to just leave. ‚ÄúCome on,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúThere has to be something I can do.‚Äù Amy shook her head and gave one of those ironic laughs that mean the person doesn‚Äôt think you‚Äôve got a snowball‚Äôs chance in hell. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll tell you what. Yes, there is something you can do: Volunteer at a suicide hotline. Then come talk to me. No guarantees, got it?‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúNow, seriously, go away.‚Äù My conversation with Amy kicked off my awareness of the complex and hidden subtleties of conversation, the power of certain words, the seemingly unintelligible emotional truths that so often underlie intelligible exchanges. A trap into which many fall is to take what other people say literally. I started to see that while people played the game of conversation, it was in the game beneath the game, where few played, that all the leverage lived. In our chat, I saw how the word ‚ÄúNo‚Äù‚Äîso apparently clear and direct‚Äîreally wasn‚Äôt so simple. Over the years, I‚Äôve thought back repeatedly to that conversation, replaying how Amy so quickly turned me down, again and again. But her ‚ÄúNo‚Äôs‚Äù were just the gateway to ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù They gave her‚Äîand me‚Äîtime to pivot, adjust, and reexamine, and actually created the environment for the one ‚ÄúYes‚Äù that mattered. While assigned to the JTTF, I worked with an NYPD lieutenant named Martin. He had a hard shell, and whenever asked for anything he responded with a terse negative. After I‚Äôd gotten to know him a bit, I asked him why. ‚ÄúChris,‚Äù he said, proudly, ‚Äúa lieutenant‚Äôs job is to say, ‚ÄòNo.‚Äô‚Äù At first, I thought that sort of automated response signaled a failure of imagination. But then I realized I did the same thing with my teenage son, and that after I‚Äôd said ‚ÄúNo‚Äù to him, I often found that I was open to hearing what he had to say. That‚Äôs because having protected myself, I could relax and more easily consider the possibilities. ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is the start of the negotiation, not the end of it. We‚Äôve been conditioned to fear the word ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù But it is a statement of perception far more often than of fact. It seldom means, ‚ÄúI have considered all the facts and made a rational choice.‚Äù Instead, ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is often a decision, frequently temporary, to maintain the status quo. Change is scary, and ‚ÄúNo‚Äù provides a little protection from that scariness. Jim Camp, in his excellent book, Start with NO,1 counsels the reader to give their adversary (his word for counterpart) permission to say ‚ÄúNo‚Äù from the outset of a negotiation. He calls it ‚Äúthe right to veto.‚Äù He observes that people will fight to the death to preserve their right to say ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù so give them that right and the negotiating environment becomes more constructive and collaborative almost immediately. When I read Camp‚Äôs book, I realized this was something we‚Äôd known as hostage negotiators for years. We‚Äôd learned that the quickest way to get a hostage-taker out was to take the time to talk them out, as opposed to ‚Äúdemanding‚Äù their surrender. Demanding their surrender, ‚Äútelling‚Äù them to come out, always ended up creating a much longer standoff and occasionally, actually contributed to death. It comes down to the deep and universal human need for autonomy. People need to feel in control. When you preserve a person‚Äôs autonomy by clearly giving them permission to say ‚ÄúNo‚Äù to your ideas, the emotions calm, the effectiveness of the decisions go up, and the other party can really look at your proposal. They‚Äôre allowed to hold it in their hands, to turn it around. And it gives you time to elaborate or pivot in order to convince your counterpart that the change you‚Äôre proposing is more advantageous than the status quo. Great negotiators seek ‚ÄúNo‚Äù because they know that‚Äôs often when the real negotiation begins. Politely saying ‚ÄúNo‚Äù to your opponent (we‚Äôll go into this in more depth in Chapter 9), calmly hearing ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù and just letting the other side know that they are welcome to say ‚ÄúNo‚Äù has a positive impact on any negotiation. In fact, your invitation for the other side to say ‚ÄúNo‚Äù has an amazing power to bring down barriers and allow for beneficial communication. This means you have to train yourself to hear ‚ÄúNo‚Äù as something other than rejection, and respond accordingly. When someone tells you ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù you need to rethink the word in one of its alternative‚Äîand much more real‚Äîmeanings: ‚ñ† I am not yet ready to agree; ‚ñ† You are making me feel uncomfortable; ‚ñ† I do not understand; ‚ñ† I don‚Äôt think I can afford it; ‚ñ† I want something else; ‚ñ† I need more information; or ‚ñ† I want to talk it over with someone else. Then, after pausing, ask solution-based questions or simply label their effect: ‚ÄúWhat about this doesn‚Äôt work for you?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat would you need to make it work?‚Äù ‚ÄúIt seems like there‚Äôs something here that bothers you.‚Äù People have a need to say, ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù So don‚Äôt just hope to hear it at some point; get them to say it early. PERSUADE IN THEIR WORLD I‚Äôd like to present you with a guy named Joe Businessman as he readies himself for a negotiation. You‚Äôve met him before. He‚Äôs the prepared type, with all his Getting to Yes strategies written out and memorized. And he‚Äôs more than ready to unleash them on the guy across the table. Joe pauses to look at his expensive suit in the mirror, fantasizing about the impressive things he‚Äôll say and the fancy charts and graphs that‚Äôll back up those things and leave his counterpart‚Äîhis opponent‚Äîvanquished and in defeat. He is Russell Crowe in Gladiator. He is The Man. Now allow me to let you in on a secret: None of that preparation will mean a damn thing. His negotiation style is all me, me, me, ego, ego, ego. And when the people on the other side of the table pick up those signals, they‚Äôre going to decide that it‚Äôs best to politely, even furtively, ignore this Superman . . . by saying ‚ÄúYes‚Äù! ‚ÄúHuh?‚Äù you say. Sure, the word they‚Äôll say right off is ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù but that word is only a tool to get this blowhard to go away. They‚Äôll weasel out later, claiming changing conditions, budget issues, the weather. For now, they just want to be released because Joe isn‚Äôt convincing them of anything; he‚Äôs only convincing himself. I‚Äôll let you in on a secret. There are actually three kinds of ‚ÄúYes‚Äù: Counterfeit, Confirmation, and Commitment. A counterfeit ‚Äúyes‚Äù is one in which your counterpart plans on saying ‚Äúno‚Äù but either feels ‚Äúyes‚Äù is an easier escape route or just wants to disingenuously keep the conversation going to obtain more information or some other kind of edge. A confirmation ‚Äúyes‚Äù is generally innocent, a reflexive response to a black-or-white question; it‚Äôs sometimes used to lay a trap but mostly it‚Äôs just simple affirmation with no promise of action. And a commitment ‚Äúyes‚Äù is the real deal; it‚Äôs a true agreement that leads to action, a ‚Äúyes‚Äù at the table that ends with a signature on the contract. The commitment ‚Äúyes‚Äù is what you want, but the three types sound almost the same so you have to learn how to recognize which one is being used. Human beings the world over are so used to being pursued for the commitment ‚Äúyes‚Äù as a condition to find out more that they have become masters at giving the counterfeit ‚Äúyes.‚Äù That‚Äôs what the people facing Joe Businessman are doing, dangling the counterfeit ‚Äúyes‚Äù so they can hear more. Whether you call it ‚Äúbuy-in‚Äù or ‚Äúengagement‚Äù or something else, good negotiators know that their job isn‚Äôt to put on a great performance but to gently guide their counterpart to discover their goal as his own. Let me tell you, I learned that the hard way. Two months after talking with Amy, I started answering phones for HelpLine, the crisis hotline founded by Norman Vincent Peale. The basic rule was that you couldn‚Äôt be with anybody on the phone for more than twenty minutes. If you did your job, it wasn‚Äôt going to take you longer than that to get them to a better place. We had a thick book of organizations we referred them to for help. It was a paramedic approach: patch them up and send them on their way. But people in crisis only accounted for about 40 percent of the calls we got. The majority of the calls came from frequent callers. These are highly dysfunctional people, energy vampires whom no one else would listen to anymore. We kept a list of frequent callers and when you got one, the first thing you had to do was check to see if the person had called that day, because they were only allowed one call a day. They knew it, too. A lot of times, they‚Äôd say, ‚ÄúYeah, I‚Äôm Eddie. I haven‚Äôt called yet today. Go ahead and check the list. You got to talk to me.‚Äù Since I was there primarily to learn a skill, I loved the frequent callers. They were a problem, and I loved trying to figure them out. I felt I had some talent at it. I felt like a superstar. When it came time for my performance review, they assigned me a shift supervisor named Jim Snyder. Jim was a hotline veteran and a sweetheart; the only problem was he always wanted to joke around. Jim understood that volunteer burnout was the biggest problem at a hotline, so he dedicated his time to making work fun. I became good friends with Jim. For my review, Jim waited until I got a call and went into the monitoring room where the supervisors could listen to our calls. The call was from one of my frequent clients, a cabbie with a fear of going outside and plenty of time to tell me about it. This energy vampire (his name was Daryl) launched into his shtick about how he was going to lose his house and with it his will to live if he couldn‚Äôt work. ‚ÄúSeriously, when was the last time someone tried to hurt you on the streets?‚Äù I asked. ‚ÄúWell, I mean, it‚Äôs been a long time,‚Äù Daryl said. ‚ÄúLike . . . ?‚Äù ‚ÄúI can‚Äôt really remember a date, Chris. Maybe a year, I guess.‚Äù ‚ÄúSo it‚Äôs safe to say that the outside world hasn‚Äôt been too hard on you, right?‚Äù ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Daryl said. ‚ÄúI suppose so.‚Äù We went back and forth like this for a while, as I made him admit that most of us had little to fear in the world. I was feeling good about my new skills, about listening to Daryl and then ‚ÄúCareFronting‚Äù him, which was the slightly goofy name we gave to assertively‚Äîbut caringly‚Äîresponding to frequent callers. It was all flowing, and our rapport was great. I even got Daryl to laugh a few times. By the time I was done with him, he couldn‚Äôt give me one reason not to step outside. ‚ÄúThank you, Chris,‚Äù Daryl said just before he hung up. ‚ÄúThanks for doing such a great job.‚Äù Before I went to see Jim, I leaned back in my chair and basked in that compliment. How often do you get that from a man in pain, I thought. Then I sprung up and strode toward the monitoring room, so proud I was practically buffing my nails on my shirt and patting my own back. Jim motioned me to the chair in front of him and gave me his biggest smile. I must have returned it with twice the wattage. ‚ÄúWell, Chris,‚Äù he said, still smiling. ‚ÄúThat was one of the worst calls I ever heard.‚Äù I stared at him, gape-jawed. ‚ÄúJim, did you hear Daryl congratulate me?‚Äù I asked. ‚ÄúI talked him down, man. I killed it.‚Äù Jim smiled‚ÄîI hated that smile right then‚Äîand nodded. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs one of the signs, because they should be congratulating themselves when they get off the line,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúThey don‚Äôt need to be congratulating you. That tells me you did too much. If they think you did it‚Äîif you were the guy who killed it‚Äîhow is he going to help himself? I don‚Äôt want to be harsh, but you were horrible.‚Äù As I listened to what Jim said, I felt that acid stomach rush you get when you are forced to accept that the guy dumping on you is completely right. Daryl‚Äôs response had been a kind of ‚Äúyes,‚Äù but it had been anything but a true commitment ‚Äúyes.‚Äù He‚Äôd made no promise to action. His ‚Äúyes‚Äù had been designed to make me feel good enough to leave him alone. Daryl may not have known it, but his ‚Äúyes‚Äù was as counterfeit as they came. You see, that whole call had been about me and my ego and not the caller. But the only way to get these callers to take action was to have them own the conversation, to believe that they were coming to these conclusions, to these necessary next steps, and that the voice at the other end was simply a medium for those realizations. Using all your skills to create rapport, agreement, and connection with a counterpart is useful, but ultimately that connection is useless unless the other person feels that they are equally as responsible, if not solely responsible, for creating the connection and the new ideas they have. I nodded slowly, the fight drained out of me. ‚ÄúOne of the worst calls?‚Äù I said to Jim. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right.‚Äù I worked hard at reorienting myself from that point on. I asked so many questions and read so much about it that soon they had me teaching two classes for new volunteers at HelpLine: the opening class, on active listening; and the one on CareFrontation. Got it, you say. It‚Äôs not about me. We need to persuade from their perspective, not ours. But how? By starting with their most basic wants. In every negotiation, in every agreement, the result comes from someone else‚Äôs decision. And sadly, if we believe that we can control or manage others‚Äô decisions with compromise and logic, we‚Äôre leaving millions on the table. But while we can‚Äôt control others‚Äô decisions, we can influence them by inhabiting their world and seeing and hearing exactly what they want. Though the intensity may differ from person to person, you can be sure that everyone you meet is driven by two primal urges: the need to feel safe and secure, and the need to feel in control. If you satisfy those drives, you‚Äôre in the door. As we saw with my chat with Daryl, you‚Äôre not going to logically convince them that they‚Äôre safe, secure, or in control. Primal needs are urgent and illogical, so arguing them into a corner is just going to push your counterpart to flee with a counterfeit ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù And being ‚Äúnice‚Äù in the form of feigned sympathy is often equally as unsuccessful. We live in an age that celebrates niceness under various names. We are exhorted to be nice and to respect people‚Äôs feelings at all times and in every situation. But nice alone in the context of negotiation can backfire. Nice, employed as a ruse, is disingenuous and manipulative. Who hasn‚Äôt received the short end of the stick in dealings with a ‚Äúnice‚Äù salesman who took you for a ride? If you rush in with plastic niceness, your bland smile is going to dredge up all that baggage. Instead of getting inside with logic or feigned smiles, then, we get there by asking for ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù It‚Äôs the word that gives the speaker feelings of safety and control. ‚ÄúNo‚Äù starts conversations and creates safe havens to get to the final ‚ÄúYes‚Äù of commitment. An early ‚ÄúYes‚Äù is often just a cheap, counterfeit dodge. About five months after she‚Äôd told me to ‚Äúgo away,‚Äù I stopped by Amy Bonderow‚Äôs office and told her that I‚Äôd volunteered at HelpLine. ‚ÄúYou did?‚Äù she asked, smiling with surprise. ‚ÄúI tell everybody to do that. And nobody ever does.‚Äù It turned out that Amy had started her negotiating career by volunteering at the same place. She started naming people who were now mutual friends of ours. We laughed about Jim. In a sudden shift, Amy stopped speaking and stared at me. I shifted in my shoes as she gave me the Pause. Then she smiled. ‚ÄúYou get the next position.‚Äù At that time, there were five other people aiming for the same slot, people who had psychology degrees, experience, and credentials. But I was on the road to the next hostage negotiation training course at the FBI Academy in Quantico, Virginia, ahead of everybody else. My career as a negotiator had officially begun. ‚ÄúNO‚Äù IS PROTECTION Think back to the telemarketer at the beginning of this chapter. The obvious reply to his question‚Äî‚ÄúDo you enjoy a nice glass of water?‚Äù‚Äîis ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù But all you want to do is scream, ‚ÄúNo!‚Äù After a question like that you just know the rest of the phone call is going to be painful. That, in a nutshell, distills the inherent contradictions in the values we give ‚ÄúYes‚Äù and ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù Whenever we negotiate, there‚Äôs no doubt we want to finish with a ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù But we mistakenly conflate the positive value of that final ‚ÄúYes‚Äù with a positive value of ‚ÄúYes‚Äù in general. And because we see ‚ÄúNo‚Äù as the opposite of ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù we then assume that ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is always a bad thing. Nothing could be further from the truth. Saying ‚ÄúNo‚Äù gives the speaker the feeling of safety, security, and control. You use a question that prompts a ‚ÄúNo‚Äù answer, and your counterpart feels that by turning you down he has proved that he‚Äôs in the driver‚Äôs seat. Good negotiators welcome‚Äîeven invite‚Äîa solid ‚ÄúNo‚Äù to start, as a sign that the other party is engaged and thinking. Gun for a ‚ÄúYes‚Äù straight off the bat, though, and your counterpart gets defensive, wary, and skittish. That‚Äôs why I tell my students that, if you‚Äôre trying to sell something, don‚Äôt start with ‚ÄúDo you have a few minutes to talk?‚Äù Instead ask, ‚ÄúIs now a bad time to talk?‚Äù Either you get ‚ÄúYes, it is a bad time‚Äù followed by a good time or a request to go away, or you get ‚ÄúNo, it‚Äôs not‚Äù and total focus. As an exercise, the next time you get a telemarketing call, write down the questions the seller asks. I promise you‚Äôll find that your level of discomfort correlates directly to how quickly he pushes you for ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù My colleague Marti Evelsizer was the one who first opened my eyes to why ‚ÄúNo‚Äù was better than ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù Marti was the FBI‚Äôs Crisis Negotiation Coordinator in Pittsburgh at the time. She was a dynamo and a negotiating genius, which earned her huge respect both within the Bureau and with the local police. But human beings are innately jealous, and her immediate supervisor was no exception to that rule. Her success diminished him, and that made her a threat. His jealousy got the better of him when the Pittsburgh Police Department‚Äôs Hostage Negotiation Team asked her to sit on the selection board for their new candidates. Picking her, and doing so over her boss, was an unprecedented move. So her boss decided to remove her from her position. For ignoring her regular duties, he said. But really it was for being a threat. When Marti sat down with her supervisor for her official dismissal, her options were few. He had every right to do as he pleased. Marti told me that she considered a variety of scenarios. She thought about going right at his jealousy and hashing it out, or explaining how the job would reflect well on the Bureau: ‚ÄúWould you like our office to be honored for its expertise?‚Äù But by the time she sat down with him, she had picked one of the most strongly worded ‚ÄúNo‚Äù-oriented setup questions I have ever heard. ‚ÄúDo you want the FBI to be embarrassed?‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù he answered. ‚ÄúWhat do you want me to do?‚Äù she responded. He leaned back in his chair, one of those 1950s faux-leather numbers that squeak meaningfully when the sitter shifts. He stared at her over his glasses and then nodded ever so slightly. He was in control. ‚ÄúLook, you can keep the position,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúJust go back out there and don‚Äôt let it interfere with your other duties.‚Äù And a minute later Marti walked out with her job intact. When I heard Marti do that, I was like, ‚ÄúBang!‚Äù By pushing for a ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù Marti nudged her supervisor into a zone where he was making the decisions. And then she furthered his feelings of safety and power with a question inviting him to define her next move. The important thing here is that Marti not only accepted the ‚ÄúNo‚Äù; she searched it out and embraced it. At a recent sales conference, I asked the participants for the one word they all dread. The entire group yelled, ‚ÄúNo!‚Äù To them‚Äîand to almost everyone‚Äî‚ÄúNo‚Äù means one thing: end of discussion. But that‚Äôs not what it means. ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is not failure. Used strategically it‚Äôs an answer that opens the path forward. Getting to the point where you‚Äôre no longer horrified by the word ‚ÄúNo‚Äù is a liberating moment that every negotiator needs to reach. Because if your biggest fear is ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù you can‚Äôt negotiate. You‚Äôre the hostage of ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù You‚Äôre handcuffed. You‚Äôre done. So let‚Äôs undress ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù It‚Äôs a reaffirmation of autonomy. It is not a use or abuse of power; it is not an act of rejection; it is not a manifestation of stubbornness; it is not the end of the negotiation. In fact, ‚ÄúNo‚Äù often opens the discussion up. The sooner you say ‚ÄúNo,‚Äù the sooner you‚Äôre willing to see options and opportunities that you were blind to previously. Saying ‚ÄúNo‚Äù often spurs people to action because they feel they‚Äôve protected themselves and now see an opportunity slipping away. Since I‚Äôve demystified ‚ÄúNo‚Äù for myself, I‚Äôve found the ideas, perceptions, and baggage that people have with that two-letter word to be fascinating. To me, it‚Äôs like watching a movie or a music video from the 1980s for the umpteenth time. You can identify with the experience‚Äîwhile simultaneously being conscious of the fact that the world, and you, have moved on. Today, I coach my students to learn to see ‚ÄúNo‚Äù for what it is. Rather than harming them or those they negotiate with, ‚ÄúNo‚Äù protects and benefits all parties in an exchange. ‚ÄúNo‚Äù creates safety, security, and the feeling of control. It‚Äôs a requirement to implementable success. It‚Äôs a pause, a nudge, and a chance for the speaker to articulate what they do want. As you can see, ‚ÄúNo‚Äù has a lot of skills. ‚ñ† ‚ÄúNo‚Äù allows the real issues to be brought forth; ‚ñ† ‚ÄúNo‚Äù protects people from making‚Äîand lets them correct‚Äîineffective decisions; ‚ñ† ‚ÄúNo‚Äù slows things down so that people can freely embrace their decisions and the agreements they enter into; ‚ñ† ‚ÄúNo‚Äù helps people feel safe, secure, emotionally comfortable, and in control of their decisions; ‚ñ† ‚ÄúNo‚Äù moves everyone‚Äôs efforts forward. One of my grad school students, a political fund-raiser named Ben Ottenhoff, drove home this lesson with a bang. For years, he‚Äôd been using a traditional ‚ÄúYes pattern‚Äù fund-raising script to raise money for Republican congressional candidates. FUND-RAISER: Hello, can I speak with Mr. Smith? MR. SMITH: Yes, this is he. FUND-RAISER: I‚Äôm calling from the XYZ Committee, and I wanted to ask you a few important questions about your views on our economy today.
